\section{GL--Brazovskii Analogy: What Is Useful and What Is Not}

\section{Core statement}

The useful content of the Ginzburg--Landau (GL) analogy is not a literal equation reversal,
but the control-parameter dependent instability analysis at the level of the quadratic kernel.

\section{1. Structural similarity}

Both GL theory and Brazovskii-type theory belong to the Landau class of order-parameter models.
Their common logic is:

\[
\text{phase transition}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\text{loss of positivity of the quadratic Hessian/kernel}.
\]

For GL,
\[
F_{\mathrm{GL}}[\psi]
=
\int d^dx\,
\left[
\alpha |\psi|^2
+
\frac{\beta}{2}|\psi|^4
+
K|(\nabla-iqA)\psi|^2
+\cdots
\right].
\]

For Brazovskii,
\[
F_{\mathrm{B}}[\Phi]
=
\int d^dx\,
\left[
r |\Phi|^2
+
Z |\nabla \Phi|^2
+
Y |\nabla^2 \Phi|^2
+
\frac{u}{2}|\Phi|^4
+\cdots
\right],
\qquad
Z<0,\quad Y>0.
\]

\section{2. Essential difference}

The two theories are not related by a simple sign flip.

GL typically describes an instability at
\[
k=0,
\]
whereas Brazovskii describes an instability at a finite shell
\[
|k|=q_*,
\qquad
q_*^2=-\frac{Z}{2Y}.
\]

Therefore the correct distinction is

\[
k=0 \text{ condensation}
\quad\neq\quad
|k|=q_* \text{ shell condensation}.
\]

Hence one should not claim
\[
\text{GL} \longrightarrow \text{TECT/Brazovskii}
\]
by a mere reversal of coefficients.

\section{3. Correct TECT use of the analogy}

Let \(X\) be a control parameter, such as stress, defect density, injected energy, or softening strength.
Then the correct quadratic kernel is

\[
\lambda(k;X)=r(X)+Z(X)k^2+Y(X)k^4.
\]

The critical point is determined by

\[
\min_k \lambda(k;X_c)=0.
\]

If \(Z(X_c)<0\) and \(Y(X_c)>0\), the minimizing shell is

\[
q_*^2(X_c)=-\frac{Z(X_c)}{2Y(X_c)},
\]
and the critical condition becomes

\[
r(X_c)-\frac{Z(X_c)^2}{4Y(X_c)}=0.
\]

This is the rigorous TECT analogue of a critical-threshold condition.

\section{4. What is justified}

The following statement is justified:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The GL analogy is useful as a critical-kernel methodology for TECT.}
}
\]

In particular, one may define:
\begin{itemize}
\item critical stress,
\item critical softening,
\item critical defect density,
\item critical injected energy,
\end{itemize}
by solving
\[
\min_k \lambda(k;X_c)=0.
\]

\section{5. What is not justified}

The following stronger claims are not justified at the current stage:

\[
\text{``GL solutions can be mirror-reversed into a TECT engine formula.''}
\]

\[
\text{``Cooper pairs are directly equivalent to topological solitons.''}
\]

\[
\text{``Flux pinning directly becomes TECT pinning.''}
\]

\[
\text{``Agreement with the Planck temperature would prove TECT.''}
\]

These are, at present, heuristic analogies rather than theorem-level statements.

\section{6. UV melting scale}

It is natural to expect that TECT should possess some UV cutoff or melting scale,
but this cannot yet be identified with the Planck temperature without an explicit derivation of

\[
a_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
\omega_{\max},
\qquad
E_{\mathrm{melt}},
\qquad
\text{and the UV-complete microscopic branch}.
\]

Therefore the correct statement is only:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT may admit a UV melting scale, but its quantitative identification remains open.}
}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The GL analogy is useful in TECT only at the level of instability methodology.

The rigorous route is to introduce a control-dependent Brazovskii kernel
\[
\lambda(k;X)=r(X)+Z(X)k^2+Y(X)k^4,
\]
and define the critical threshold by
\[
\min_k \lambda(k;X_c)=0.
\]

If \(Z(X_c)<0\) and \(Y(X_c)>0\), then
\[
q_*^2(X_c)=-\frac{Z(X_c)}{2Y(X_c)},
\qquad
r(X_c)-\frac{Z(X_c)^2}{4Y(X_c)}=0.
\]

Thus the correct TECT use of the GL analogy is not literal equation reversal,
but critical-kernel analysis for shell instability.

Claims such as
\[
\text{GL solution} \to \text{engine formula},
\qquad
T_{\mathrm{melt}}=T_{\mathrm{Planck}},
\]
are not justified at the current stage.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Critical Stress and Critical Softening Criteria in a TECT--Brazovskii Effective Theory}

\section{Goal}

The useful content of the GL analogy is not a literal equation reversal,
but the control-parameter dependent instability criterion at the level of the quadratic kernel.

We therefore formulate the TECT critical-threshold condition directly in the Landau--Brazovskii language.

\section{1. Effective quadratic kernel}

Let \(X\) denote a control parameter.
Depending on the application, \(X\) may represent:
\[
X=\sigma
\quad\text{(stress)},
\qquad
X=\chi
\quad\text{(softening strength)},
\qquad
X=n_d
\quad\text{(defect density)},
\qquad
X=\mathcal E
\quad\text{(injected energy density)}.
\]

Consider the quadratic part of the effective free energy:
\[
F_2[\Phi;X]
=
\int \frac{d^dk}{(2\pi)^d}\,
\lambda(k;X)\,|\Phi_k|^2,
\]
with
\[
\lambda(k;X)=r(X)+Z(X)k^2+Y(X)k^4,
\qquad
Y(X)>0.
\]

The phase boundary is determined by the loss of positivity of the quadratic kernel:
\[
\min_{k\ge 0}\lambda(k;X_c)=0.
\]

\section{2. Two distinct instability channels}

There are two qualitatively different possibilities.

\subsection{Case A: zero-momentum instability}

If
\[
Z(X_c)\ge 0,
\]
then the minimum occurs at
\[
k=0,
\]
and the criticality condition is simply
\[
r(X_c)=0.
\]

This is the ordinary \(k=0\) Landau-type instability.

\subsection{Case B: finite-shell (Brazovskii) instability}

If
\[
Z(X_c)<0,
\qquad
Y(X_c)>0,
\]
then the minimum occurs at a nonzero shell
\[
q_*^2(X_c)=-\frac{Z(X_c)}{2Y(X_c)}.
\]

Substituting this into \(\lambda\), one obtains
\[
\lambda_{\min}(X)
=
r(X)-\frac{Z(X)^2}{4Y(X)}.
\]

Hence the finite-shell instability threshold is
\[
\boxed{
r(X_c)-\frac{Z(X_c)^2}{4Y(X_c)}=0,
\qquad
Z(X_c)<0,
\qquad
Y(X_c)>0.
}
\]

This is the correct TECT analogue of a critical-threshold condition.

\section{3. Critical stress criterion}

Let \(X=\sigma\) be an applied stress.
Then the effective coefficients become stress-dependent:
\[
r=r(\sigma),
\qquad
Z=Z(\sigma),
\qquad
Y=Y(\sigma).
\]

The critical stress is defined by
\[
\boxed{
\min_k \lambda(k;\sigma_c)=0.
}
\]

For the shell-instability regime this becomes
\[
\boxed{
r(\sigma_c)-\frac{Z(\sigma_c)^2}{4Y(\sigma_c)}=0,
\qquad
Z(\sigma_c)<0,
\qquad
Y(\sigma_c)>0.
}
\]

Equivalently, the stress-driven shell wave number at threshold is
\[
q_*^2(\sigma_c)=-\frac{Z(\sigma_c)}{2Y(\sigma_c)}.
\]

Thus \(\sigma_c\) is the point at which the stressed medium first becomes unstable to finite-wavevector shell formation or shell collapse, depending on the branch interpretation.

\section{4. Critical softening criterion}

Let \(X=\chi\) denote a softening parameter.
This may represent any microscopic effect that reduces stiffness or increases susceptibility to shell formation.

Then
\[
r=r(\chi),
\qquad
Z=Z(\chi),
\qquad
Y=Y(\chi),
\]
and the critical softening threshold is
\[
\boxed{
r(\chi_c)-\frac{Z(\chi_c)^2}{4Y(\chi_c)}=0,
\qquad
Z(\chi_c)<0,
\qquad
Y(\chi_c)>0.
}
\]

If \(\chi\) is interpreted as a direct reduction of the effective stiffness,
then \(\chi_c\) is the point where the medium first loses quadratic stability against finite-shell fluctuations.

\section{5. Exact solvable linear-response parametrization}

A useful parametrization is
\[
r(X)=r_0-aX,
\qquad
Z(X)=Z_0-bX,
\qquad
Y(X)=Y_0,
\qquad
Y_0>0.
\]

Then the shell-instability threshold is given by
\[
r_0-aX_c-\frac{(Z_0-bX_c)^2}{4Y_0}=0.
\]

Multiplying by \(4Y_0\), one gets
\[
4Y_0 r_0-4Y_0 a X_c-(Z_0-bX_c)^2=0,
\]
or
\[
b^2X_c^2+(4Y_0 a-2bZ_0)X_c+(Z_0^2-4Y_0 r_0)=0.
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
X_c
=
\frac{
-(4Y_0 a-2bZ_0)
\pm
\sqrt{(4Y_0 a-2bZ_0)^2-4b^2(Z_0^2-4Y_0 r_0)}
}{
2b^2
}.
}
\]

The physically relevant root is the first one reached along the chosen control direction, subject to
\[
Z(X_c)<0.
\]

\section{6. Weak-control expansion}

If the control dependence is weak and \(X_c\) is close to a reference point \(X=0\), define
\[
\Delta(X):=r(X)-\frac{Z(X)^2}{4Y(X)}.
\]

Then
\[
\Delta(X_c)=0.
\]

Expanding to first order,
\[
\Delta(X)\approx \Delta(0)+X\,\Delta'(0),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
X_c\approx -\frac{\Delta(0)}{\Delta'(0)}.
}
\]

Now
\[
\Delta'(X)
=
r'(X)
-\frac{Z(X)Z'(X)}{2Y(X)}
+\frac{Z(X)^2Y'(X)}{4Y(X)^2}.
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
X_c
\approx
-\frac{
r_0-\dfrac{Z_0^2}{4Y_0}
}{
r_0'
-\dfrac{Z_0 Z_0'}{2Y_0}
+\dfrac{Z_0^2Y_0'}{4Y_0^2}
},
}
\]
where
\[
r_0=r(0),\quad Z_0=Z(0),\quad Y_0=Y(0),
\]
and
\[
r_0'=r'(0),\quad Z_0'=Z'(0),\quad Y_0'=Y'(0).
\]

This gives the leading shift of the instability threshold under a weak control parameter.

\section{7. Local threshold in an inhomogeneous medium}

If the control parameter is spatially varying, \(X=X(x)\), the local shell-instability condition becomes
\[
\lambda_{\min}(x)
=
r(X(x))-\frac{Z(X(x))^2}{4Y(X(x))}.
\]

A local instability region is then defined by
\[
\boxed{
r(X(x))-\frac{Z(X(x))^2}{4Y(X(x))}<0,
\qquad
Z(X(x))<0,
\qquad
Y(X(x))>0.
}
\]

This is the natural criterion for a stress hotspot, defect hotspot, or injected-energy hotspot to trigger local softening, shell collapse, or local melting of the ordered branch.

\section{8. Honest interpretation}

The criterion derived above is rigorous only at the level of an effective Landau--Brazovskii description.

It does not, by itself, prove:
\begin{itemize}
\item the full UV-complete TECT dynamics,
\item a literal GL\(\to\)TECT equation reversal,
\item or an engine-level extraction law.
\end{itemize}

What it does provide is the correct control-dependent instability criterion for finite-shell ordering or finite-shell collapse in a TECT-type effective theory.

\section{Final form}

The most compact and useful final statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT critical threshold}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\min_k\lambda(k;X_c)=0.
}
\]

For the Brazovskii shell regime,
\[
\boxed{
r(X_c)-\frac{Z(X_c)^2}{4Y(X_c)}=0,
\qquad
q_*^2(X_c)=-\frac{Z(X_c)}{2Y(X_c)}.
}
\]

\section{Final Summary}

The useful GL-to-TECT transfer is not a literal reversal of equations,
but the instability criterion based on the quadratic kernel.

For a TECT--Brazovskii effective theory with control parameter \(X\),
\[
\lambda(k;X)=r(X)+Z(X)k^2+Y(X)k^4,
\qquad
Y(X)>0,
\]
the critical threshold is defined by
\[
\min_k\lambda(k;X_c)=0.
\]

In the finite-shell regime \(Z(X_c)<0\), the critical point is
\[
q_*^2(X_c)=-\frac{Z(X_c)}{2Y(X_c)},
\]
and the threshold condition becomes
\[
r(X_c)-\frac{Z(X_c)^2}{4Y(X_c)}=0.
\]

This formula gives the correct effective-theory definition of:
\[
\text{critical stress},\qquad
\text{critical softening},\qquad
\text{critical defect density},\qquad
\text{critical injected energy density}.
\]

It is the rigorous content of the GL analogy at the Landau--Brazovskii level.

\section{A Corrected GL-Style Completion Route for the TECT Defect Sector}

\section{1. Honest status}

The GL program provides a useful methodological template:
identify an order parameter, write an effective free energy,
derive the field equations by variation, construct nontrivial solutions,
and compare their observable consequences with experiment.

However, this is only a template.
It does not imply that TECT automatically acquires the same theorem-level status as GL
merely by analogy.

At present, the final complete UV-to-IR TECT theory is not yet closed,
the Einstein-type gravity sector remains open,
the gauge sector remains open,
the chiral matter/family sector is only conditional,
and the decoupling of extra states is still open.

Therefore the defect-to-dark-matter route must be formulated as a genuine derivation problem,
not as a rhetorical analogy.

\section{2. Correct TECT order parameter}

The natural TECT order-parameter variables are not the GL superconducting field itself,
but the condensate/projector pair
\[
\Phi(x)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1.
\]

A minimal effective free energy has the form
\[
F_{\mathrm{TECT}}[\Phi,P]
=
F_{\mathrm{Braz}}[\Phi]
+
F_{\mathrm{geom}}[P]
+
F_{\mathrm{mix}}[\Phi,P]
+
F_{\mathrm{defect}}.
\]

The Brazovskii core is schematically
\[
F_{\mathrm{Braz}}[\Phi]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
r|\Phi|^2
+
Z|\nabla\Phi|^2
+
Y|\nabla^2\Phi|^2
+
\frac{u}{2}|\Phi|^4
\right],
\qquad
Z<0,\quad Y>0,
\]
while the projector sector has the schematic form
\[
F_{\mathrm{geom}}[P]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
\frac{\kappa_P}{2}\Tr(\partial_iP\,\partial_iP)
+\cdots
\right].
\]

\section{3. Defect background as the real analogue of the GL vortex step}

In GL theory, the decisive nontrivial object is the vortex solution.
In TECT, the corresponding decisive object is a topological defect background
\[
(\Phi_{\mathrm{def}},P_{\mathrm{def}}).
\]

The mathematically relevant problem is:
find a finite-energy defect solution satisfying the Euler--Lagrange equations
\[
\frac{\delta F_{\mathrm{TECT}}}{\delta \Phi}=0,
\qquad
\frac{\delta F_{\mathrm{TECT}}}{\delta P}=0,
\]
with nontrivial topological boundary conditions.

This is the correct TECT replacement of the GL vortex step.

\section{4. Linearized defect operator}

Once a defect background is found, one studies the linearized operator
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{def}}
=
\frac{\delta^2 F_{\mathrm{TECT}}}{\delta(\Phi,P)^2}\Bigg|_{(\Phi_{\mathrm{def}},P_{\mathrm{def}})}.
\]

The key questions are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Does the defect support localized low-energy modes?
\item Are these modes chiral/protected?
\item Is there a finite defect mass/tension?
\item Is the asymptotic bulk spectrally gapped?
\end{enumerate}

These are the true structural questions behind any claim that defects generate matterlike sectors.

\section{5. Defect-to-dark-matter criterion}

A TECT defect sector can behave as dark matter only if the following conditions hold.

\subsection{(D1) Stability}
There must exist a conserved or effectively conserved defect charge \(Q_{\mathrm{def}}\)
such that the defect lifetime is cosmologically long:
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{decay}}\approx 0
\quad\text{or at least}\quad
\tau_{\mathrm{def}}\gg H_0^{-1}.
\]

\subsection{(D2) Finite mass}
Each isolated defect must have finite energy
\[
M_{\mathrm{def}}
=
\int d^3x\,\mathcal E_{\mathrm{def}}(x)
<\infty.
\]

\subsection{(D3) Cold-medium behavior}
At large scales, the defect ensemble must admit an effective stress tensor of dust-like form
\[
T^{\mu\nu}_{\mathrm{def}}
\approx
\rho_{\mathrm{def}}\,u^\mu u^\nu,
\qquad
p_{\mathrm{def}}\ll \rho_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]

Equivalently, the effective equation-of-state parameter must satisfy
\[
w_{\mathrm{def}}:=\frac{p_{\mathrm{def}}}{\rho_{\mathrm{def}}}\approx 0.
\]

\subsection{(D4) Correct abundance}
The defect abundance must be generated by some formation mechanism,
for example freeze-out of defect pairs, Kibble-type formation, or defect-network fragmentation,
such that
\[
\rho_{\mathrm{def}}(t_0)
=
M_{\mathrm{def}}\,n_{\mathrm{def}}(t_0)
\]
matches the observed dark-sector energy density.

\subsection{(D5) Weak visible coupling}
The defect sector must not overcouple to the visible sector.
Schematically,
\[
\Gamma(\text{visible}\leftrightarrow \text{defect})
\]
must be sufficiently suppressed in the late universe.

\section{6. Minimal theorem target}

The correct theorem target is therefore not
\[
\text{``defect = dark matter''}
\]
as an immediate slogan,
but the following conditional statement:

\begin{quote}
If TECT admits a finite-energy, cosmologically stable defect family
whose coarse-grained stress tensor is dust-like
and whose abundance matches the dark-sector density,
then the corresponding defect ensemble defines a dark-matter candidate sector.
\end{quote}

In formulas, the minimal target is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT defect dark sector}
\Longleftarrow
(D1)\wedge(D2)\wedge(D3)\wedge(D4)\wedge(D5).
}
\]

\section{7. What would count as a decisive TECT analogue of the GL vortex success?}

The true GL-vortex-style decisive step for TECT would be:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an explicit topological defect solution,
\item a controlled linearized defect operator,
\item a finite defect mass/tension,
\item a dust-like coarse-grained stress tensor,
\item and at least one quantitatively distinctive observable consequence.
\end{enumerate}

That would be the correct TECT analogue of the GL vortex success,
not an engine formula.

\section{Final Summary}

The GL completion path can be used in TECT only as a methodological scaffold.

The correct TECT translation is:

\[
\text{order parameter}
\to
\text{effective free energy}
\to
\text{topological defect background}
\to
\text{linearized defect operator}
\to
\text{coarse-grained cosmological sector}.
\]

Thus the mathematically honest dark-matter route is not
\[
\text{``defect = dark matter''}
\]
as a slogan,
but the conditional program:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finite-energy defect}
\wedge
\text{stability}
\wedge
w_{\mathrm{def}}\approx 0
\wedge
\text{correct abundance}
\wedge
\text{weak visible coupling}
}
\]
\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
\text{TECT defect sector is a dark-matter candidate.}
}
\]

This is the correct TECT analogue of the decisive nontrivial-solution step
played by the vortex in GL theory.

\section{Two-Track Numerical Program: Reference Spectral Backend and Auxiliary BCC Backend}

\section{Current stage}

The numerical program should now be split into two parallel tracks.

The first track keeps the current pseudospectral working backend as the reference acceptance backend.
The second track introduces a BCC-adapted auxiliary backend for UV-sensitive branch-finding and shell-retention comparison.

Thus the correct numerical strategy is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{Track A: reference spectral backend for certificate-level validation,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Track B: auxiliary BCC-adapted backend for branch-finding and UV-sensitive comparison.}
}
\]

\section{Track A: unchanged reference backend}

Track A keeps the current continuum/spectral stiffness variables
\[
|k|^2,
\qquad
|k|^4,
\]
and therefore the same operational working PDE and the same extractor logic.

Its role is:
\begin{itemize}
\item certificate-level validation of the current working branch,
\item controlled comparison between grids,
\item continuity with the already established shell/Bloch/projector extraction chain.
\end{itemize}

\section{Track B1: immediate compatible BCC-symbol backend}

Define the positive BCC stiffness symbol
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)
=
\frac{8}{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}^2}
\left[
1-
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_x}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_y}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_z}{2}\right)
\right].
}
\end{equation}

Its small-momentum expansion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)
=
|k|^2 + O\!\left(a_{\mathrm{BCC}}^2|k|^4\right).
}
\end{equation}

Thus the BCC-adapted replacement of the continuum stiffness variables is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|k|^2 \longrightarrow \Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k),
\qquad
|k|^4 \longrightarrow \Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)^2.
}
\end{equation}

This preserves the current solver sign convention because only the positive stiffness variables are being replaced.

\section{Why Track B1 is honest but still auxiliary}

Track B1 is honest because it inserts a BCC-sensitive UV symbol.
However, it is still auxiliary rather than final because it is not yet the exact real-space nearest-neighbor BCC finite-difference operator on a bipartite BCC lattice.

The current field representation and extractor are still formulated on the cubic periodic FFT grid.
Therefore Track B1 is the correct immediate comparison backend, but not yet the final exact BCC lattice backend.

\section{Track B2: exact real-space BCC nearest-neighbor backend}

The exact real-space nearest-neighbor BCC Laplacian is naturally bipartite.
Thus it requires a two-sublattice field representation
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Psi = (\Psi_A,\Psi_B),
}
\end{equation}
with body-centered nearest-neighbor coupling between the two interpenetrating sublattices.

This is not a trivial drop-in replacement of the current backend because:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the field storage changes,
\item the linear operator changes,
\item the shell projector and Bloch extraction must be reformulated for the new lattice representation.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the exact real-space BCC nearest-neighbor backend should be treated as a later Track-B2 project.

\section{What should be compared first}

The first comparison between Track A and Track B1 should not attempt an immediate full certificate extraction.
The correct first comparison layer is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{residual decay},
\qquad
\text{energy drift},
\qquad
\text{shell fraction},
\qquad
\text{orbit balance}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final summary}

The TECT numerical program should now proceed in two tracks.
Track A preserves the current pseudospectral backend for certificate-level validation.
Track B introduces BCC sensitivity in two stages:
\begin{enumerate}
\item an immediate compatible BCC-symbol backend on the same FFT grid,
\item a later exact two-sublattice real-space BCC backend.
\end{enumerate}

The immediate mathematical replacement is
\[
|k|^2 \to \Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k),
\qquad
|k|^4 \to \Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)^2,
\]
with
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)
=
\frac{8}{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}^2}
\left[
1-
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_x}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_y}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_z}{2}\right)
\right].
\]

This is the correct honest first BCC-sensitive backend that can be compared directly with the current reference solver without rewriting the entire acceptance pipeline.

\section{Final Summary}

The numerical TECT program should proceed in two parallel tracks.

Track A keeps the current pseudospectral backend as the operational reference backend for certificate-level validation.

Track B introduces BCC sensitivity in two stages:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Track B1: immediate compatible BCC-symbol backend}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Track B2: later exact two-sublattice BCC real-space backend}
}
\]

The immediate BCC-sensitive replacement is
\[
|k|^2 \to \Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k),

with

\]

This Track-B1 backend is the correct immediate auxiliary backend for comparing branch-finding diagnostics without breaking the current validation pipeline.

\section{First Solver-Level A/B Comparison: Spectral Backend vs BCC-Symbol Backend}

We compared the reference spectral backend (Track A) and the BCC-symbol auxiliary backend (Track B1)
under the same
\[
64^3,\qquad
L=16.0,\qquad
\text{steps}=400,\qquad
dt=10^{-3},
\]
using the same initialized working branch.

For Track A, the residual changed as
\[
1.38108265\times 10^{-2}
\;\to\;
1.38472654\times 10^{-2},
\]
so the residual drifted upward.
The energy changed as
\[
1.49499076\times 10^2
\;\to\;
1.50503446\times 10^2.
\]

For Track B1, the residual changed as
\[
1.36645291\times 10^{-2}
\;\to\;
1.35938303\times 10^{-2},
\]
so the residual decreased.
The energy changed as
\[
1.47181523\times 10^2
\;\to\;
1.47618527\times 10^2.
\]

Therefore, under identical solver conditions, the BCC-symbol backend exhibits
\[
\boxed{
\text{actual residual decrease}
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\text{smaller energy drift}
}
\]
compared with the reference spectral backend.

Hence the correct conclusion is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{Track B1 is numerically preferable to Track A at the solver level.}
}
\]

However, the improvement is still moderate rather than dramatic.
Thus the BCC-symbol backend should be regarded as a promising auxiliary branch-finding backend,
not yet as a fully established replacement of the reference acceptance backend.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Track-B1 Extractor Verdict}

The BCC-symbol auxiliary backend improves the solver-level drift behavior,
but the current extractor fails on the resulting branch because the reduced effective quadratic slot becomes
\[
M_2<0.
\]

In the present run,
\[
M_2=-2.0473490321375274\times 10^{-4},
\]
so the branch cannot be accepted by the current extractor as a physically admissible reduced carrier branch.

Therefore the honest conclusion is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Track B1 is useful as an auxiliary branch-finding backend,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{it is not yet compatible with the current acceptance/extraction pipeline as a drop-in replacement of the reference spectral backend.}
}
\]

\section{Recommended Two-Track Next Step}

\begin{enumerate}
\item Keep Track A as the operational reference backend for certificate-level extraction.
\item Keep Track B1 only as a solver-level auxiliary backend for branch-finding diagnostics.
\item Do not use the current extractor output on Track B1 as an acceptance-level physical result.
\item Introduce, if desired, an interpolation backend
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{mix}}(k)
=
(1-\varepsilon)|k|^2+\varepsilon\,\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k),
\qquad 0\le \varepsilon\le 1,
\]
and test where the extracted branch first loses \(M_2>0\).
\end{enumerate}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Track-B1 Recalibration Strategy}

The BCC-symbol backend should not be inserted as an immediate full replacement of the reference spectral backend.

The correct next step is a calibrated interpolation:
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda_{\mathrm{mix}}(k)
=
(1-\varepsilon)|k|^2+\varepsilon\,\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k),
\qquad
0\le \varepsilon \le 1.
}
\]

The purpose is not to force the fully BCC-sensitive backend immediately,
but to determine the largest admissible BCC admixture for which the current extraction pipeline still remains physically admissible.

The practical calibration targets are:
\[
\boxed{
M_2>0,
\qquad
\text{acceptable residual drift},
\qquad
\text{nonpathological }m_*,g_{\mathrm{eff}}.
}
\]

Thus the first calibration variable is \(\varepsilon\),
while the BCC lattice scale \(a_{\mathrm{BCC}}\) should be adjusted only after a viable \(\varepsilon\)-window is found.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Numerical-Methodology Clarification: Avoiding Circularity in the BCC Claim}

A serious methodological distinction must be made between two different numerical roles:

\begin{enumerate}
\item unbiased emergence tests from isotropic dynamics, and
\item branch-continuation / branch-validation numerics.
\end{enumerate}

These are not the same.

If a simulation uses a BCC-biased backend, such as a BCC-symbol Laplacian or a mixed BCC-sensitive stiffness symbol, then that simulation cannot be used as direct evidence that BCC order emerges spontaneously from fully isotropic microscopic dynamics.

Likewise, if the initial condition is not a truly random field but rather a preconstructed branch seed plus small noise, then the simulation is a branch-continuation calculation rather than an unbiased spontaneous-emergence test.

Therefore the following methodological rule is mandatory:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Any claim of spontaneous BCC emergence from noise must be supported only by}
\ \texttt{laplacian\_mode=spectral}
\ \text{together with a truly random initial field.}
}
\]

By contrast, simulations using
\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=bcc\_symbol}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=mixed\_bcc}
\]
should be described only as auxiliary UV-sensitive branch-finding or branch-stabilization diagnostics.

Accordingly, if Section 8.2 currently relies on branch-seeded runs or BCC-sensitive backends, then its present wording should be weakened.
The correct statement would be that a BCC-compatible branch is numerically continued or stabilized, not that BCC order is proven to emerge spontaneously from unbiased isotropic noise.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Numerical provenance control}

All numerical runs now record the initialization provenance explicitly.
In particular, the solver metadata distinguishes between
\[
\texttt{pure\_noise},
\quad
\texttt{seed\_plus\_noise},
\quad
\texttt{corr\_seed},
\quad
\texttt{bcc\_seed},
\quad
\texttt{external}
\]
initialization modes, and also records whether the backend stiffness is purely spectral or BCC-biased.

Accordingly, the boolean flag
\[
\texttt{emergence\_claim\_safe}
\]
is set to true only when
\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode}=\texttt{spectral}
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\texttt{init\_mode}=\texttt{pure\_noise}.
\]

Thus branch-continuation runs and unbiased emergence tests are no longer conflated at the metadata level.

\section{Reference backend and BCC-sensitive backend}

The reference solver/backend infrastructure is still the current executable working branch,
not the unique final complete UV TECT solver. This is explicitly how the current solver and PDE status are framed in the project notes. 

The minimal microscopic core that remains fixed is still the finite-\(q\) Brazovskii class,
with shell-forming dispersion
\[
\omega^2(k)=\mu^2+Z|k|^2+Y|k|^4,

Z<0,\quad Y>0,
\qquad

This shell-instability requirement is the reason the minimal microscopic TECT model lies in the Brazovskii class. :contentReference[oaicite:1]{index=1}

\section{BCC-sensitive backend hierarchy}

The BCC-sensitive stiffness symbol used in the auxiliary backend is
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)
=
\frac{8}{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}^2}
\left[
1-
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_x}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_y}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_z}{2}\right)

The immediate mixed-BCC backend is
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{mix}}(k)
=
(1-\varepsilon)|k|^2+\varepsilon\,\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k),
\qquad
0\le \varepsilon \le 1.

This was introduced because the pure BCC-sensitive auxiliary backend initially improved solver-level drift behavior but did not reliably remain compatible with the current extraction pipeline.
Hence the honest next step was not an immediate full replacement, but a calibrated interpolation.

\section{Solver-level recalibration scan}

A solver-only recalibration scan was run on the mixed backend at fixed
\[
64^3,\qquad

\text{steps}=300,\qquad

\]
for
\[
\varepsilon\in\{0.0,0.1,0.2,0.35,0.5,0.7,1.0\}.

The final residuals decreased monotonically as \(\varepsilon\) increased:
\[
r_{\mathrm{final}}(0.0)=0.0138288011,
\qquad
r_{\mathrm{final}}(1.0)=0.0136889981.

Likewise, the final energies decreased monotonically:
\[
E_{\mathrm{final}}(0.0)=150.2020634,
\qquad

Thus the mixed-BCC interpolation improves the solver-level probe monotonically as the BCC admixture is increased.

However, the solver-level gain is moderate rather than dramatic, and therefore the decisive question is not solver monotonicity but extractor admissibility.

\section{Mixed-BCC extraction-admissible family}

The extractor has now been run successfully for three nontrivial mixed-BCC points:

\subsection{Point 1: \(\varepsilon=0.35\)}

The extractor completed successfully and yielded
\[
W_0=8,
\qquad
W_2=-1,
\qquad
M_2=1.2650137493350785\times 10^{-1},
\]
\[
G_4=3.9837762718282943,
\qquad
m_*^2=1.5812671866688482\times 10^{-2},
\qquad
m_*=1.2574844677644526\times 10^{-1},
\]
\[
g_{\mathrm{eff}}=4.9797203397853679\times 10^{-1},
\qquad
\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=-3.75\times 10^{-1},
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{cub}}=1.0337628517261523\times 10^{-1}.
\]
This is the first confirmed extraction-admissible mixed-BCC point. :contentReference[oaicite:2]{index=2}

\subsection{Point 2: \(\varepsilon=0.7\)}

The extractor again completed successfully and yielded
\[
W_0=8,
\qquad
W_2=-1,
\qquad
M_2=1.0534154905837770\times 10^{-1},
\]
\[
G_4=3.7216649515256801,
\qquad
m_*^2=1.3167693632297212\times 10^{-2},
\qquad
m_*=1.1475057138113610\times 10^{-1},
\]
\[
g_{\mathrm{eff}}=4.6520811894071001\times 10^{-1},
\qquad
\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=-3.75\times 10^{-1},
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{cub}}=1.1872683863527496\times 10^{-1}.
\]

\subsection{Point 3: \(\varepsilon=1.0\)}

A crucial revision occurred here.
Contrary to the earlier tentative interpretation, the extractor also completed successfully at the endpoint
\[
\varepsilon=1.0,
\]
with
\[
W_0=8,
\qquad
W_2=-1,
\qquad
M_2=1.0458598426364185\times 10^{-1},
\]
\[
G_4=4.9169557314598933,
\qquad
m_*^2=1.3073248032955231\times 10^{-2},
\qquad
m_*=1.1433830518664877\times 10^{-1},
\]
\[
g_{\mathrm{eff}}=6.1461946643248666\times 10^{-1},
\qquad
\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=-3.75\times 10^{-1},
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{cub}}=2.0948661249315439\times 10^{-1}.
\]

Therefore the previous tentative claim
\[
0.7<\varepsilon_*<1
\]
must be withdrawn for the current mixed-backend implementation.

\section{Revised mixed-BCC verdict}

The correct updated statement is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{The mixed-BCC extraction-admissible family reaches the full endpoint } \varepsilon=1.0
\text{ at the current }64^3\text{ protocol.}
}
\]

So the program is no longer in a phase of searching for the admissibility boundary \(\varepsilon_*\).
Instead, it is now in the phase of choosing an operational point within the admissible family
\[
\varepsilon\in\{0.35,0.7,1.0\}.
\]

The honest practical interpretation is:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\varepsilon=0.35\): most conservative extractor-stable mixed-BCC point;
\item \(\varepsilon=0.7\): balanced mixed-BCC operational point;
\item \(\varepsilon=1.0\): maximally BCC-sensitive admissible point within the current mixed implementation.
\end{itemize}

\section{Current recommended operational choice}

At the present stage, the recommended operational labeling is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{main working mixed-BCC point: } \varepsilon=0.7
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{conservative reference mixed-BCC point: } \varepsilon=0.35
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{BCC-heavy comparison point: } \varepsilon=1.0
}
\]

This recommendation is not because \(\varepsilon=1.0\) fails.
It is because \(\varepsilon=0.7\) appears to offer a more balanced compromise between BCC sensitivity and moderate quartic/cubic deformation,
whereas \(\varepsilon=1.0\) produces larger
\[
G_4,\qquad g_{\mathrm{eff}},\qquad Z_{\mathrm{cub}}.
\]

\section{Methodology audit: circularity risk}

A major methodology clarification is now mandatory.

The current solver is not, by default, an unbiased spontaneous-emergence solver.
If no external initialization is provided, the original solver starts from
\[
\Psi_0=\Psi_{\mathrm{corr\_seed}}+\text{noise},
\]
rather than from a truly random field.
This is visible in the solver implementation. :contentReference[oaicite:3]{index=3}

Hence the current executable solver is fundamentally a
\[
\boxed{
\text{working-branch continuation / validation solver}
}
\]
rather than a direct proof of spontaneous BCC emergence from unbiased isotropic noise.

Likewise, if one uses
\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=bcc\_symbol}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=mixed\_bcc},
\]
then one is introducing a BCC-sensitive backend at the operator level.
Such runs cannot be used as direct evidence that BCC order emerges spontaneously from completely isotropic microscopic dynamics.

Therefore the correct rule is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{A claim of spontaneous BCC emergence from noise is methodologically safe only if}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=spectral}
\quad\text{and}\quad
\texttt{init\_mode=pure\_noise}.
}
\]

\section{Solver/extractor provenance issue}

Because of the above circularity risk, solver metadata and initialization provenance must be made explicit.

The original solver metadata records backend path, grid, steps, residual, and energy,
but does not explicitly record all methodology-critical initialization provenance fields. :contentReference[oaicite:4]{index=4}

Hence the next code-maintenance task is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item solver must explicitly record
\[
\texttt{init\_mode},\quad
\texttt{noise\_kind},\quad
\texttt{noise\_scale},\quad
\texttt{uses\_branch\_seed},\quad
\texttt{uses\_pure\_random\_init},
\]
together with
\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode},\quad
\texttt{is\_bcc\_biased\_backend},\quad
\texttt{emergence\_claim\_safe}.
\]

\item extractor should read source metadata and print/store methodology warnings:
\[
\text{spectral vs BCC-biased},
\qquad
\text{pure noise vs branch seed},
\qquad
\text{emergence-safe vs continuation-only}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

The purpose is to ensure that no future subsection can accidentally conflate
\[
\text{unbiased emergence test}
\]
with
\[
\text{branch continuation test}.
\]

\section{Current honest interpretation of Section 8.2-type claims}

The current numerical pipeline supports the following strong but honest statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a BCC-compatible branch can be numerically continued, stabilized, and extracted under the current working branch infrastructure.}
}
\]

But the current pipeline does \emph{not yet} justify the stronger statement

\[
\boxed{
\text{BCC order is proven to emerge spontaneously from unbiased isotropic noise.}
}
\]

unless the run is explicitly demonstrated to use
\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=spectral}
\quad\text{and}\quad
\texttt{init\_mode=pure\_noise}.
\]

Thus all future writing must keep these two claims sharply separated.

\section{Connection to the broader TECT program}

This numerical stage does not replace the main microscopic TECT program.
The broad theorem-level target remains the carrier-acceptance / mass-protection chain
and the actual finite coefficient extraction from the working microscopic branch. That remains the main frontier in the project notes. 

The mixed-BCC numerical program is therefore best understood as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{an auxiliary numerical branch-calibration and methodology-control layer}
}
\]

sitting alongside the main microscopic acceptance and coefficient-extraction program.

\section{What is closed now}

The following statements are now justified:

\begin{enumerate}
\item mixed-BCC solver-level performance improves monotonically with increasing \(\varepsilon\);
\item the mixed-BCC extraction-admissible family includes at least
\[
\varepsilon=0.35,\qquad \varepsilon=0.7,\qquad \varepsilon=1.0;
\]
\item the earlier tentative claim
\[
0.7<\varepsilon_*<1
\]
is no longer valid for the current mixed-backend implementation;
\item the present program has exposed a genuine methodology risk:
current executable branch-continuation numerics must not be misrepresented as unbiased spontaneous-emergence evidence;
\item provenance-aware solver/extractor infrastructure is now a necessary part of the numerical program.
\end{enumerate}

\section{What remains open}

The following are still open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a true unbiased spectral+\(\)pure-noise emergence test with explicit methodology-safe metadata;
\item selection of the final operational mixed-BCC point among
\[
\varepsilon=0.35,\ 0.7,\ 1.0;
\]
\item possible comparison of shell fraction / orbit balance / extraction robustness across those operational points;
\item eventual migration from Track B1/mixed-BCC to Track B2 exact two-sublattice real-space BCC backend;
\item integration of provenance-safe numerics into the paper text so that no circularity claim can arise.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Immediate Next Task}

The most nonredundant next step is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{finalize provenance-safe solver/extractor metadata}
}
\]

together with

\[
\boxed{
\text{choose and document the operational mixed-BCC point}
}
\]

under the honest classification:

\[
\text{continuation/validation backend}
\neq
\text{unbiased spontaneous-emergence backend}.
\]

Concretely, the immediate next numerical/documentation tasks are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item freeze the operational comparison set
\[
\varepsilon=0.35,\qquad \varepsilon=0.7,\qquad \varepsilon=1.0;
\]
\item compare them as
\[
\text{conservative / balanced / BCC-heavy};
\]
\item update the solver so that initialization provenance and noise provenance are explicit in metadata;
\item update the extractor so that provenance warnings are propagated;
\item rewrite the manuscript language so that Section 8.2-type claims refer only to methodology-safe runs.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Short continuation prompt}

We are no longer searching for the mixed-BCC admissibility boundary.
That boundary has effectively reached \(\varepsilon=1.0\) in the current mixed implementation at \(64^3\).

The immediate frontier is now methodological and operational:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{freeze provenance-safe numerics and choose the operational mixed-BCC point.}
}
\end{equation}

Use the following practical labels:
\[
\varepsilon=0.35 \Rightarrow \text{conservative mixed-BCC point},
\]
\[
\varepsilon=0.7 \Rightarrow \text{balanced mixed-BCC point},
\]
\[
\varepsilon=1.0 \Rightarrow \text{BCC-heavy admissible point}.
\]

Do not claim spontaneous BCC emergence from any run that is not explicitly
\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode=spectral}
\quad\text{and}\quad
\texttt{init\_mode=pure\_noise}.
\]

The next conversation should continue from:
\[
\boxed{
\text{provenance-safe numerical infrastructure}
\;+\;
\text{paper wording correction}
\;+\;
\text{operational mixed-BCC point selection}.
}
\]

\section{TECT Microscopic PDE from Enriched Shell--Defect Carrier}

\subsection{1. UV Starting Point (Class II Action)}

We begin from the Class II UV action:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{UV}}^{(II)} =
\frac{M_X^2}{2} X_i^a X_i^a
+ \alpha X_i^a J_i^a[\Psi]
+ \beta X_i^a K_i^a[P,\psi]
\end{equation}

where:
\begin{equation}
J_i^a[\Psi] = \partial_i \Psi^a 
K_i^a[P,\psi] = (\partial_i P_B)\, \psi^a
\end{equation}

Integrating out $X_i^a$ yields:

\begin{equation}
X_i^a = -\frac{1}{M_X^2} \left(
\alpha J_i^a + \beta K_i^a
\right)
\end{equation}

Substituting back:

\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{eff}} =
- \frac{1}{2M_X^2}
\left(
\alpha J_i^a + \beta K_i^a
\right)^2
\end{equation}

---

\subsection{2. Expansion into Physical Sectors}

Expanding:

\begin{align}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{eff}} =
& -\frac{\alpha^2}{2M_X^2} (J_i^a)^2
- \frac{\beta^2}{2M_X^2} (K_i^a)^2
- \frac{\alpha\beta}{M_X^2} J_i^a K_i^a
\end{align}

Each term corresponds to:

\begin{itemize}
\item $(JJ)$: standard kinetic sector
\item $(KK)$: defect-induced mass/tension
\item $(JK)$: chiral mixing (Dirac structure)
\end{itemize}

---

\subsection{3. Projection onto Shell Carrier}

The field is decomposed as:

\begin{equation}
\Psi(x) = \sum_{A} \psi_A(x) e^{i q_A \cdot x}
\end{equation}

with BCC star:

\begin{equation}
|q_A| = q_0, \quad A = 1,\dots,12
\end{equation}

Gradient acts as:

\begin{equation}
\partial_i \Psi \rightarrow i q_{A,i} \psi_A + \partial_i \psi_A
\end{equation}

---

\subsection{4. Effective Kinetic Operator}

After shell averaging:

\begin{equation}
\langle q_{A,i} q_{A,j} \rangle = \frac{q_0^2}{3} \delta_{ij}
\end{equation}

Thus:

\begin{equation}
(JJ) \Rightarrow \lambda_\parallel (\partial_i \psi)^2
\end{equation}

with:

\begin{equation}
\lambda_\parallel = \frac{\alpha^2}{M_X^2} \frac{q_0^2}{3}
\end{equation}

---

\subsection{5. Defect Background Contribution}

For $CP^2$ defect:

\begin{equation}
P_B = z_B z_B^\dagger
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\partial_i P_B =
\theta'(r) (e_i z_B^\dagger + z_B e_i^\dagger)
\end{equation}

Then:

\begin{equation}
(KK) \Rightarrow m_0^2 \psi
\end{equation}

where:

\begin{equation}
m_0^2 \sim \frac{\beta^2}{M_X^2} \int (\theta')^2 dr
\end{equation}

---

\subsection{6. Mixed Term (Dirac Structure)}

The cross term:

\begin{equation}
(JK) \sim \psi^\dagger (q \cdot \partial P_B) \psi
\end{equation}

generates antisymmetric coupling:

\begin{equation}
(JK) \Rightarrow i \beta_{\mathrm{eff}} \, \epsilon_{ijk} \partial_j \psi_k
\end{equation}

with:

\begin{equation}
\beta_{\mathrm{eff}} \sim \frac{\alpha\beta}{M_X^2} q_0 \theta'
\end{equation}

---

\subsection{7. Final Microscopic PDE}

Collecting all terms:

\begin{equation}
\rho \, \partial_t^2 \psi =
\lambda_\parallel \nabla(\nabla \cdot \psi)
- \alpha_{\mathrm{eff}} \nabla^2 \psi
+ i \beta_{\mathrm{eff}} (\nabla \times \psi)
- m_0^2 \psi
\end{equation}

where:

\begin{align}
\lambda_\parallel &= \frac{\alpha^2}{M_X^2} \frac{q_0^2}{3} \\
\alpha_{\mathrm{eff}} &= \text{(transverse stiffness from loop renormalization)} \\
\beta_{\mathrm{eff}} &= \frac{\alpha\beta}{M_X^2} q_0 \theta' \\
m_0^2 &= \frac{\beta^2}{M_X^2} \int (\theta')^2 dr
\end{align}

---

\subsection{8. Interpretation}

The equation has the structure:

\begin{itemize}
\item longitudinal elastic mode
\item transverse wave propagation
\item chiral (Dirac-like) mixing
\item defect-induced mass gap
\end{itemize}

This is the minimal closed-form microscopic PDE emerging from:

\begin{equation}
\text{TECT condensate (BCC)} + \text{CP}^2 \text{ defect} + \text{Class II UV}
\end{equation}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Code Audit of the Final TECT Numerical Pipeline}

\subsection{Scope}

We audited the final numerical pipeline consisting of

\[
\texttt{real\_backend\_pt\_bcc\_mixed\_FINAL.py},
\qquad
\texttt{tect\_solver\_pt\_FINAL.py},
\qquad
\texttt{tect\_actual\_extractor\_pt\_FINAL.py}.
\]

The purpose of the audit is not to claim a final unconditional UV-complete TECT simulator,
but to determine whether the present executable working branch is internally consistent
at the level of:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the microscopic residual operator,
\item the IMEX solver splitting,
\item the free-energy monitor,
\item the observable extraction layer.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Backend status}

The backend implements the working microscopic branch

\[
R[\Psi]
=
\left(r + Z\,\Delta_{\mathrm{eff}} + Y\,\Delta_{\mathrm{eff}}^2\right)\Psi
+
R_{\mathrm{fam}}[\Psi]
+
R_{\mathrm{lock}}[\Psi]
+
R_{\mathrm{shell}}[\Psi]
+
R_{\mathrm{nl}}[\Psi]
+
R_{\mathrm{Class\,II}}[\Psi].
\]

The effective stiffness symbol is correctly defined by

\[
s_2(k)=
\begin{cases}
k^2, & \text{spectral mode},\\[1mm]
\hat\Delta_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k), & \text{bcc\_symbol mode},\\[1mm]
(1-\varepsilon)k^2+\varepsilon \hat\Delta_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k), & \text{mixed\_bcc mode},
\end{cases}
\]

with

\[
\hat\Delta_{\mathrm{BCC}}(k)
=
\frac{8}{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}^2}
\left[
1-
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_x}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_y}{2}\right)
\cos\!\left(\frac{a_{\mathrm{BCC}}k_z}{2}\right)
\right].
\]

This gives the correct quadratic symbol

\[
L(k)=r+Z\,s_2(k)+Y\,s_2(k)^2.
\]

Moreover, the quadratic free-energy term is now correctly restored as

\[
F_{\mathrm{quad}}
=
\frac{r}{2}\int |\Psi|^2,
\]

so the energy monitor is consistent with the residual-level quadratic action.

\subsection{Solver consistency}

The decisive previous numerical inconsistency was the mismatch between the implicit IMEX denominator
and the actual microscopic quadratic operator in mixed-BCC mode.

In the corrected final version, the implicit denominator is built from the same stiffness symbol \(s_2\)
as the backend residual itself:

\[
\widehat{\Psi}^{\,n+1}
=
\frac{
\widehat{\Psi}^{\,n}
-
\Delta t\,\widehat{N[\Psi^n]}
}{
1+\Delta t\left(r+Z\,s_2+Y\,s_2^2+\eta_{\mathrm{shell}}(|k|-q_0)^2\right)
}.
\]

Therefore the IMEX fixed point is now aligned with the actual zero of the residual:

\[
R[\Psi_*]=0.
\]

Equally importantly, the shell-bias term is no longer double-counted.
It appears only in the implicit linear denominator and is removed from the explicit remainder.
Hence the corrected splitting is structurally consistent.

\subsection{Extractor status}

The extractor computes patchwise low-mode masses \(m_\alpha^2\) and quartic coefficients \(g_\alpha\),
then forms the raw moments

\[
W_0,\qquad W_2,\qquad M_2,\qquad G_4,
\]

and the effective observables

\[
m_*^2=\frac{M_2}{W_0},
\qquad
m_*=\sqrt{\frac{M_2}{W_0}},
\qquad
g_{\mathrm{eff}}=\frac{G_4}{W_0}.
\]

At the code-audit level, the extraction logic is acceptable.
However, one quantity must not be overinterpreted:

\[
\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=\frac{3W_2}{W_0}.
\]

For the default \(8\)-patch geometry (two polar patches and six equatorial patches),
together with the default identity family projector, one has identically

\[
W_0=8,
\qquad
W_2=-1,
\qquad
\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=-\frac{3}{8}.
\]

Thus \(\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=-3/8\) is a geometric identity of the patch construction,
not a dynamical observable and not evidence of a physical fixed point.

\subsection{Remaining systematic limitation}

The main remaining numerical systematic comes from patch overlap.
At \(N=64\), the default angular half-width is sufficiently large that neighboring equatorial patches
can overlap in momentum space.
Therefore the patchwise quantities \(m_\alpha^2\) and \(g_\alpha\) can inherit a controllable
but nonzero angular-window bias.

This does not automatically invalidate the averaged observables \(m_*\) and \(g_{\mathrm{eff}}\),
but it does mean that patch-width stability tests remain part of the honest numerical protocol.

\subsection{Final audit statement}

The final code audit yields the following honest conclusion.

\begin{enumerate}
\item The previously critical IMEX inconsistency in mixed-BCC mode has been corrected.
\item The shell-bias double counting has been removed.
\item The quadratic free-energy normalization has been corrected.
\item The extractor provenance logic and interpretation warning for \(\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}\) are now explicit.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the final uploaded codebase is internally consistent at the level of the present
\emph{working microscopic branch}.

What is still not justified is any claim stronger than that:
the code should be cited as an executable working-branch TECT simulator with audited provenance,
not yet as a final unconditional full-UV TECT completion.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Architecture Upgrade Path from the Current Working Branch to a More Complete TECT Code}

\section{Goal}

The present numerical codebase is already a materially improved and internally audited
\emph{working microscopic branch} implementation.
However, it is not yet the final complete TECT simulator.
The correct next task is therefore not cosmetic optimization,
but an architectural upgrade from

\[
\text{working-branch residual solver}
\]

to

\[
\text{actual microscopic operator-audit simulator}.
\]

The upgrade target is the ability to compute, from the actual converged branch data,

\[
L(k)
\;\longrightarrow\;
K_i:=\partial_{k_i}L(k)\big|_{G_*}
\;\longrightarrow\;
P_*K_iP_*
\;\longrightarrow\;
(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta),
\]

together with the corresponding remote-gap and carrier-admissibility certificates.

\section{Current status}

The current final codebase already closes the following numerical consistency layer:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the backend residual uses the correct BCC-sensitive stiffness symbol \(s_2(k)\);
\item the IMEX solver denominator now matches the backend linear operator;
\item shell-bias double counting has been removed;
\item the quadratic free-energy coefficient \(r\) has been restored;
\item the extractor now records provenance and explicitly warns that
\(\epsilon_{\mathrm{lock}}=-3/8\) is a geometric identity of the default patch construction,
not a dynamical observable.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the current code should be regarded as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{an internally consistent audited working-branch simulator.}
}
\]

What it still does \emph{not} provide is the full microscopic audit of the theorem-level carrier data.

\section{Upgrade principle}

The correct upgrade principle is:



do not merely make the current solver larger;
\quad
instead, promote the code so that it computes the actual theorem-target quantities.



In practice, this means the next code-generation stage must be centered on
\emph{operator extraction}, not only on field evolution.

\section{Stage U1: Consolidation of the current audited working branch}

Before adding new physics sectors, the current codebase should be normalized into a single coherent package.

\subsection{U1.1. File-format and provenance normalization}

The export version string, metadata conventions, and extractor expectations should be made fully uniform.
In particular, the package format tag, config version, and metadata fields should be synchronized so that no ambiguity remains between nominal format labels and actual output structure.

\subsection{U1.2. Automated reproducibility layer}

The solver should support built-in ensemble runs:
\[
\{\text{seed}_a\}_{a=1}^{N_{\mathrm{ens}}}
\]
with automatic collection of
\[
m_*,
\qquad
g_{\mathrm{eff}},
\qquad
\text{residual history},
\qquad
\text{energy history}.
\]
This is necessary because the current pipeline is still primarily single-run oriented.

\subsection{U1.3. Patch-window stability scan}

The extractor should automatically repeat the observable extraction for
\[
d\theta_{\mathrm{patch}}\in\{d\theta_1,d\theta_2,\dots\}
\]
and report stability windows for
\[
m_*,
\qquad
g_{\mathrm{eff}},
\qquad
m_\alpha^2,
\qquad
g_\alpha.
\]
This is required because the current default \(N=64\) patch setting still allows angular overlap systematics.

\section{Stage U2: Actual Bloch-operator extraction}

This is the most important upgrade.

\subsection{U2.1. Background linearization module}

Given a converged branch \(\Psi_0\), the code must build the actual linearized operator
\[
L_{\Psi_0}(k).
\]
This should not remain implicit inside a residual callback only.
Rather, it must become an explicit computational object that can be interrogated spectrally.

\subsection{U2.2. Folded shell basis construction}

For the selected shell pair \(\pm G_*\), construct the explicit shell-relevant folded basis
\[
\mathcal H_D^{(N)}
\cong
\mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{valley}}\otimes \mathbb C^2_{\mathrm{int}}.
\]
This basis must be generated directly from the numerical background,
not only from the abstract analytic reduced model.

\subsection{U2.3. Low-slot projector construction}

The code must compute a low-energy projector
\[
P_*.
\]
This may be implemented by one of the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item contour-integral spectral projector;
\item explicitly selected low-eigenspace projector;
\item stabilized block-Schur projection on the shell-relevant carrier space.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{U2.4. Derivative-resolved projected transport matrices}

The code must then evaluate
\[
K_i:=\partial_{k_i}L(k)\big|_{k=G_*}
\]
and project them as
\[
M_i=P_*K_iP_*.
\]
From these projected matrices one can directly extract
\[
\lambda_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha,
\qquad
\beta,
\]
through the already-derived trace formulas.

\subsection{U2.5. Output layer}

The extractor output should be extended from
\[
(m_*,g_{\mathrm{eff}},m_\alpha^2,g_\alpha)
\]
to the actual theorem-target carrier data:
\[
\Delta_{00},
\qquad
u_\parallel,
\qquad
m_\parallel,
\qquad
L_*,
\qquad
\lambda_\parallel,
\qquad
\alpha,
\qquad
\beta.
\]

\section{Stage U3: Basis-level carrier audit}

The next required upgrade is to move from averaged observables to actual carrier certification.

\subsection{U3.1. Construction of a microscopic transport basis}

Let
\[
V_{\mathrm{TECT}}=\mathrm{span}\{E_A\}_{A=1}^d
\]
be the actual numerically extracted transport space around the converged branch.

\subsection{U3.2. Target overlaps}

The code must compute the three overlap families
\[
\ell_{\parallel A},
\qquad
\ell_{IA},
\qquad
\ell_{JA},
\]
where
\[
\ell_{\parallel A}=\langle T^{(\parallel)},E_A\rangle,
\qquad
\ell_{IA}=\langle T^{(I)},E_A\rangle,
\qquad
\ell_{JA}=\langle T^{(J)},E_A\rangle.
\]

\subsection{U3.3. Existence certificate}

The actual carrier audit should then report not only averaged coefficients,
but the existence-type certificate
\[
\exists A:\ \ell_{\parallel A}\neq 0,
\qquad
\exists B:\ (\ell_{IB},\ell_{JB})\neq (0,0).
\]
This is the numerically honest form of the theorem-target carrier survival test.

\section{Stage U4: Remote-sector closure}

A more complete code must also verify that the desired low carrier is isolated.

\subsection{U4.1. Non-enriched benchmark block}

Construct the same-shell non-enriched and remote benchmark blocks explicitly.

\subsection{U4.2. Finite gap lower bound}

Compute the benchmark gap
\[
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{fin}}
\]
on the relevant momentum window.

\subsection{U4.3. Residual correction bound}

Estimate the residual remote correction size
\[
\eta_{R,\rho}.
\]

\subsection{U4.4. Remote-gap certificate}

The code should then produce the explicit inequality report
\[
\Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{fin}}>\eta_{R,\rho}.
\]
This upgrades the current low-mode analysis into a genuine isolation certificate.

\section{Stage U5: Promotion of field content}

The present backend still treats the Class II sector effectively.
A more complete code must eventually promote the missing microscopic sectors to actual dynamical variables.

\subsection{U5.1. Projector/geometry sector}

The code should promote either
\[
z(x),\qquad z^\dagger z=1,
\]
or
\[
P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger,
\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P,
\]
to an explicit field sector rather than treating orientation only through a fixed locked direction.

\subsection{U5.2. Heavy carrier field}

Likewise, the Class II mediator field
\[
X_i^a
\]
should ultimately be represented explicitly as a dynamical field.
This is not necessary for the next upgrade stage, but it is necessary for a genuine final full-field simulator.

\subsection{U5.3. Coupled solver target}

The final long-term numerical target is therefore a coupled solver on
\[
(\Psi,P,X)
\quad\text{or minimally}\quad
(\Psi,z,X).
\]

\section{Stage U6: Methodology split between emergence and branch continuation}

The current provenance-aware metadata layer is already a strong step forward.
A more complete code should now make the split operational.

\subsection{U6.1. Emergence protocol}

For emergence-style claims, the code should automatically enforce:

\[
\texttt{laplacian\_mode = spectral},
\qquad
\texttt{init\_mode = pure\_noise},
\]
with ensemble repetition and attractor statistics.

\subsection{U6.2. Branch-validation protocol}

For branch continuation or BCC-sensitive validation runs, the code should use a separate result class and a separate metadata tag, so that the two methodologies cannot be conflated downstream.

\section{Priority order}

The correct development order is not arbitrary.

\subsection{Immediate next priority}

The immediate next priority is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage U2: actual Bloch-operator extraction.}
}
\]

This is because the current main open problem is no longer mere solver consistency,
but the actual extraction of
\[
(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta)
\]
from the microscopic operator.

\subsection{Second priority}

The second priority is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage U3 + U4: carrier audit and remote-gap closure.}
}
\]

\subsection{Final priority}

Only after that does it become optimal to build the full coupled-field simulator:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage U5: explicit }(\Psi,P,X)\text{ dynamics.}
}
\]

\section{Final conclusion}

The present codebase should be understood as a corrected and audited \emph{working branch}.
To make it more complete, one should not first enlarge the nonlinear solver indiscriminately.
The correct next step is to make the code compute the actual theorem-target microscopic data:

\[
L(k)\to P_*K_iP_*\to(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta),
\]

then certify the carrier basis and remote gap, and only afterward promote the remaining
effective sectors into fully dynamical coupled fields.

In short:

\[
\boxed{
\text{next: operator audit,}
\qquad
\text{then: carrier/remote certification,}
\qquad
\text{finally: full coupled TECT field solver.}
}
\]

\section{Blueprint of the Final Full Coupled TECT Field Solver}

\subsection{Layer 1: Full field dynamics}
\begin{align}
\texttt{01\_field\_config.py} &:\ \text{global parameters, constraints, discretization setup} \\
\texttt{02\_geometry\_sector.py} &:\ z(x),\,P(x)=zz^\dagger,\ \text{projector / defect geometry dynamics} \\
\texttt{03\_matter\_sector.py} &:\ \Psi(x)\in\mathbb{C}^3\ \text{core condensate dynamics} \\
\texttt{04\_mediator\_sector.py} &:\ X_i^a(x)\ \text{explicit Class II heavy field dynamics} \\
\texttt{05\_coupled\_residual.py} &:\ R_\Psi,\ R_P,\ R_X\ \text{full coupled residual assembly} \\
\texttt{06\_coupled\_solver.py} &:\ \text{time evolution / gradient flow / IMEX / Newton-Krylov}
\end{align}

\subsection{Layer 2: Microscopic operator audit}
\begin{align}
\texttt{07\_bloch\_linearization.py} &:\ L_{\Phi_0}(k)\ \text{around converged background} \\
\texttt{08\_projector\_spectral.py} &:\ P_*\ \text{low-slot spectral projector construction} \\
\texttt{09\_transport\_extractor.py} &:\ K_i=\partial_{k_i}L|_{G_*},\ M_i=P_*K_iP_* \\
\texttt{10\_remote\_gap\_audit.py} &:\ \Delta_{\mathrm{bench},\rho}^{\mathrm{fin}},\ \eta_{R,\rho}
\end{align}

\subsection{Layer 3: Theorem-target certification}
\begin{align}
\texttt{11\_carrier\_audit.py} &:\ \ell_{\parallel A},\ \ell_{IA},\ \ell_{JA} \\
\texttt{12\_observable\_report.py} &:\ (\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta),\ m_*,g_{\mathrm{eff}},\ \text{final certificates}
\end{align}

\subsection{Layer 4: Provenance and reproducibility}
\begin{align}
\texttt{13\_run\_manager.py} &:\ \text{single/ensemble runs, seed control, restart logic} \\
\texttt{14\_provenance.py} &:\ \text{metadata, emergence-safe tagging, branch-vs-emergence separation} \\
\texttt{15\_stability\_scan.py} &:\ \text{patch-width scan, parameter scan, convergence scan} \\
\texttt{16\_package\_export.py} &:\ \text{standardized export/import of all solver/audit artifacts}
\end{align}

\section{Full Technical Specification of the Coupled TECT Field Solver}

\section{Global Data Structures}

We define the core field bundle:

\begin{align}
\Phi(x)
=
\Big(
\Psi(x)\in\mathbb{C}^3,\;
z(x)\in\mathbb{C}^3,\;
X_i^a(x)\in\mathbb{R}
\Big)
\end{align}

with constraints:

\begin{align}
z^\dagger z = 1,\qquad
P = z z^\dagger,\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P.
\end{align}

All modules exchange data via the structured object:

\begin{align}
\texttt{State} = \{\Psi, z, P, X, \nabla \Psi, \nabla z, \dots\}
\end{align}

and parameter object:

\begin{align}
\texttt{Config} = \{r,Z,Y,\lambda,\gamma,\alpha_X,\beta_X,M_X,\dots\}
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 01: field\_config.py}

\subsection{Purpose}

Central parameter registry.

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{load\_config()} &\to \texttt{Config} \\
\texttt{build\_grid(Config)} &\to \texttt{Grid}
\end{align}

\subsection{Output}

\[
\texttt{Config},\quad \texttt{Grid}
\]

---

\section{Module 02: geometry\_sector.py}

\subsection{Mathematical Role}

Implements

\begin{align}
P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger,\qquad
\nabla_i P,\quad [P,\partial_i P]
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{init\_z(Grid)} &\to z(x) \\
\texttt{project\_z(z)} &\to z/||z|| \\
\texttt{compute\_P(z)} &\to P \\
\texttt{geom\_grad(P)} &\to \partial_i P
\end{align}

\subsection{Constraint Enforcement}

\begin{align}
z \leftarrow \frac{z}{||z||}
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 03: matter\_sector.py}

\subsection{Core Operator}

\begin{align}
R_\Psi
=
(r + Z s_2 + Y s_2^2)\Psi
+ R_{\mathrm{fam}} + R_{\mathrm{lock}} + R_{\mathrm{nl}}
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{residual\_psi(State, Config)} &\to R_\Psi \\
\texttt{energy\_psi(State)} &\to F_\Psi
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 04: mediator\_sector.py}

\subsection{Field}

\begin{align}
X_i^a(x)
\end{align}

\subsection{Residual}

\begin{align}
R_{X_i^a}
=
M_X^2 X_i^a
+
\alpha_X J_i^a[\Psi]
+
\beta_X K_i^a[P,\Psi]
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{residual\_X(State)} &\to R_X
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 05: coupled\_residual.py}

\subsection{Full Residual}

\begin{align}
R_{\mathrm{full}}
=
(R_\Psi,\ R_z,\ R_X)
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{assemble\_residual(State)} &\to R_{\mathrm{full}}
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 06: coupled\_solver.py}

\subsection{Evolution}

\begin{align}
\Phi^{n+1}
=
\Phi^n - \Delta t\,R_{\mathrm{full}}(\Phi^n)
\end{align}

(IMEX / Newton-Krylov optional)

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{solve(Config)} &\to \Phi_*
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 07: bloch\_linearization.py}

\subsection{Operator}

\begin{align}
L_{\Phi_*}(k)
=
\frac{\delta R}{\delta \Phi}\Big|_{\Phi_*}
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{build\_linear\_operator(State)} &\to L(k)
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 08: projector\_spectral.py}

\subsection{Projector}

\begin{align}
P_* = \sum_{n\in \mathrm{low}} |u_n\rangle\langle u_n|
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
\texttt{compute\_projector(L)} &\to P_*
\end{align}

---

\section{Module 09: transport\_extractor.py}

\subsection{Transport Operators}

K_i = \partial_{k_i}L(k)\big|_{G_*}
\end{align}

\begin{align}
M_i = P_* K_i P_*
\end{align}

\subsection{Coefficients}

\begin{align}
\lambda_\parallel,\quad \alpha,\quad \beta
\end{align}

\subsection{API}

\begin{align}
extract\_transport(L, P_*) \to (\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta)
\end{align}

---

\end{align}
